property="articleSection">research-article</div><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D31441%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYuelei%2BPan%252C%2BXudong%2BCheng%252C%2BLunlun%2BGong%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D37%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b11984%26title%3DDouble-Morphology%2BCoS2%2BAnchored%2Bon%2BN-Doped%2BMultichannel%2BCarbon%2BNanofibers%2Bas%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D31451%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2018" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11984p_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11984&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Na-ion batteries (NIBs) have attracted increasing attention given the fact that sodium is relatively more plentiful and affordable than lithium for sustainable and large-scale energy storage systems. However, the shortage of electrode materials with outstanding comprehensive properties has limited the practical implementations of NIBs. Among all the discovered anode materials, transition-metal sulfide has been proven as one of the most competitive and promising ones due to its excellent redox reversibility and relatively high theoretical capacity. In this study, double-morphology N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>/multichannel carbon nanofibers composites (CoS<sub>2</sub>/MCNFs) are precisely designed, which overcome common issues such as the poor cycling life and inferior rate performance of CoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes. The conductive 3D interconnected multichannel nanostructure of CoS<sub>2</sub>/MCNFs provides efficient buffer zones for the release of mechanical stresses from Na<sup>+</sup> ions intercalation/deintercalation. The synergy of the diverse structural features enables a robust frame and a rapid electrochemical reaction in CoS<sub>2</sub>/MCNFs anode, resulting in an impressive long-term cycling life of 900 cycles with a capacity of 620 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> (86.4% theoretical capacity) and a surprisingly high-power output. The proposed design in this study provides a rational and novel thought for fabricating electrode materials.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D31441%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYuelei%2BPan%252C%2BXudong%2BCheng%252C%2BLunlun%2BGong%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D37%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b11984%26title%3DDouble-Morphology%2BCoS2%2BAnchored%2Bon%2BN-Doped%2BMultichannel%2BCarbon%2BNanofibers%2Bas%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D31451%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Composites" class="article__tags__link">Composites</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Ions" class="article__tags__link">Ions</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290920&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanoparticles" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanoparticles" class="article__tags__link">Nanoparticles</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cobalt+Disulfide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cobalt%20disulfide">cobalt disulfide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Transition-metal+Sulfide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_transition-metal%20sulfide">transition-metal sulfide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon+Nanofibers&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbon%20nanofibers">carbon nanofibers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon+Nanofibers&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbon%20nanofibers">carbon nanofibers</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">In the last couple of years, with the fast development of electric vehicles and soaring popularity of portable electronic devices, electrical energy storage technologies have attracted increasing attention from both academia and industry. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> Among those storage systems, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) and Li-ion batteries (LIBs) are both essential systems, because of their excellent ion storage capacity and superior cycle life. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6,7)</a> However, due to the shortage and unclear distribution of lithium resources, the production cost of LIBs keeps increasing. It is critically significant and urgent to develop an innovative rechargeable battery as an alternative.</div><div class="NLM_p">NIBs are an attractive choice due to the plentiful resources of sodium on the earth, which can serve as a new low-cost energy storage technology. Although the working mechanisms of Na-ion batteries are expected to be analogous to those of Li-ion batteries, unfortunately the normally used materials for LIBs are not well suited for Na<sup>+</sup> ion intercalation and deintercalation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a> Therefore, there are still challenges lying ahead with regard to seeking appropriate electrode materials for rapid Na-ion diffusion and repeated intercalation and deintercalation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10,11)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">So far, a variety of electrode materials for NIBs, such as transition-metal sulfides, transition-metal diseleniums, hard carbon materials, and metal oxides have been investigated. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 12 to 18">(12−18)</a> Among these, transition-metal sulfides (e.g., FeS<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>, SnS, CoS<sub>2</sub>, and so on) with their great potential for Na ion storage have received increasing attention because of their many advantages: (1) a smaller bandgap energy than the metal–oxygen bond, resulting in a relatively higher electrochemical activity; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> (2) rapid Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion velocity; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> and (3) high theoretical capacity and good thermal stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 to 24">(22−24)</a> CoS<sub>2</sub> (existing in nature as cattierite) has been widely regarded as a potential semiconductor with lots of application aspects, like supercapacitors, the catalyst for hydrogen evolution, solar batteries, and LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 25 to 29">(25−29)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">However, CoS<sub>2</sub> as an electrode material applied in NIBs, has previously been less frequently investigated. Indeed, in spite of its excellent electrochemical features and high theoretical sodium ion storage capacity (greater than 800 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), its long cycling stability and rate performance are disappointing, which is mainly because of the large volume expansion/contraction when repeated charging/discharging. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 30 to 32">(30−32)</a> All of these have prohibited its wide acceptance as an anode in NIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">So far, efforts have been made to optimize the cycling life of CoS<sub>2</sub>. One of the effective methods is to load the CoS<sub>2</sub> on a highly conductive carbon substrate to improve the electrical conductivity. For example, after combining CoS<sub>2</sub> particles with multiwalled carbon nanotubes (MWCNTs) or graphene sheets, the composite shows a relatively higher and enhanced specific capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> In addition, downsizing the particles of CoS<sub>2</sub> to optimize the surface effect and reduce the distance of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion is also a useful way of benefiting Na<sup>+</sup> ions storage and cycling stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Recently, our research group also demonstrated that spherical nanoscale CoS<sub>2</sub> anchored in the carbon nanofibers presents an outstanding cycling stability and a high rate discharge performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Recently, heteroatoms doped (e.g., born, nitrogen, and phosphorus) in the electrode materials have been shown as a superior method to improve the electrode cycling performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref37" aria-label="reference 31 and 37">(31,37)</a> Among those materials, nitrogen is the most widely and intensively investigated heteroatom, which is beneficial to generate more active sites and optimize surface wettability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> For instance, Lin et al. found that CoS<sub>2</sub>/NiS<sub>2</sub> core–shell nanocubes show excellent lithium/sodium ion storage capacities after coating the N-doped carbon layer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> However, the reported CoS<sub>2</sub>/carbon composites in the literature still show less than satisfactory long-term cycling life, which may derive from their unreliable architecture and poor nanostructure strength. Therefore, it is imperative and critical to improve their electrochemical property such as by tailoring their structures and adjusting surface engineering.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, in this study, we designed N-doped double-morphology CoS<sub>2</sub>/multichannel carbon nanofiber composites (CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs) that exhibit remarkably long cycling stability and rate performance when serving as anode materials for NIBs. This unique design well integrates three critical design fundamentals resulting in outstanding electrochemical performance for Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage: (1) Multichannel carbon nanofibers act as a suitable carbon frame and are not only able to increase the electronic conductivity of the composites, but also sufficiently tolerate the repeated volume variation, thereby steadying the structure during the electrochemical reaction process. (2) CoS<sub>2</sub> crystals are controlled at the nanoscale and assembled into double morphologies, namely nanoparticle and nanosheets, respectively, which are helpful for surface effects on the active materials and reducing the distance of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion as well as for electrolytic penetration. (3) The nitrogen doped heteroatom introduces far more active cites for the extra Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage and enhances the surface wettability. Consequently, the N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites deliver a high specific capacity and outstanding cycling life of 900 cycles with extremely stable capacity retention for NIBs. Furthermore, the Na-ion full batteries assembled with CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C-BN as electrodes present a large reversible capacity reaching up to 348 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when tested at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Preparation of Multichannel Carbon Nanofibers (MCNFs)</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Following a typical procedure, 10.25 g DMF (<i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide) with 2.5 wt % polystyrene was mixed and moderately stirred around 5 h. After that, the solution was filled with 1.22 g PAN (polyacrylonitrile) followed with mechanically stirring for about 12 h. The original nanofibers (PAN/PS) were prepared by means of electrospinning, applied with cathode–anode distance, voltage, and feeding flow rate fixed at 10 cm, 15 kV, and 8.37 μL/min, respectively. Next, the obtained original nanofibers were cut into small pieces and preoxidized in air around 3 h with a constant temperature of 280 °C followed with heat treatment under the temperature of 800 °C for another 2 h in argon atmosphere for further carbonization.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Preparation of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Generally, Co(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O and thiourea at a molar ratio of 1:1 were filled into 70 mL EG (ethylene glycol), and the mixed solution was continuously stirred for 1 hs. Then the prepared MCNFs pieces (∼30 mg) were mixed with the above solution with gentle stirring at 40 °C for around 1 h. Then, the solution contained MCNFs was poured into an autoclave followed with heat treatment under the temperature of 180 °C overnight. Then the as-prepared samples were washed with ethanol and then dried at 50 °C in an oven. Finally, the obtained black pieces were further heated in Ar atmosphere under 600 °C for about 5 h with sulfur powder based on a weight ratio of 3:1 for optimizing the degree of products crystallinity. For comparison, bare CoS<sub>2</sub> spheres were also obtained with the absence of MCNFs, keeping other experimental conditions unchanged.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Crystalline phases were identified by XRD (X-ray diffraction) testing technology using X-ray diffractometer with Philips X’pert, Raman spectrum testing using LabRamHR and XPS tests. FESEM (field-emission scanning electron microscopy) (SU-8220, HITACHI, Japan), TEM (transmission electron microscope) (JEM-2100F, JEOL) and HRTEM (high resolution TEM) were used to investigate morphologies of samples. TG (Thermogravimetry) was used to analyze the CoS<sub>2</sub> proportion within the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrodes were composed of the active materials, acetylene black, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF). These materials were mixed and formed a flurry at the ratio of 8:1:1 in mass. The viscous slurry was smeared on a Cu paper and then dried in the vacuum oven overnight. Coin cells were composed of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites acting as cathode and Na metal as anode. Electrolyte and separators for the NIB were chosen from 1.0 M NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>/DEGDME (diethylene glycol dimethyl ether) and Whatman, respectively. The electrochemical testing were analyzed based on a Neware batteries tester ranging from 0.4 to 2.9 V at the room temperature. The behavior of cyclic voltammetry were carried out at a low sweep rate of 0.1 mV/s. In contrast, the cells with pure MCNFs as well as pure CoS<sub>2</sub> as work electrodes were assembled and investigated under the equal experimental conditions.</div><div class="NLM_p last">For assembly of the full cells, CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs and flower-like Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C-BN were used as anode and cathode, respectively. The cathode Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C-BN are prepared on the basis of a literature. In a typical synthesized way of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C-BN, 0.72g V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, and 1.52g C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O were added into 40 mL deionized water and stirred at 70 °C for 1 h. Then, 1.84g NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O and 0.4 g glucose were added into the solution with stirring for another 10 min followed with 100 mL <i>n</i>-propanol pouring in. The obtained solution was dried in an oven for overnight. Finally the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C-BN was obtained by mixing and heating treatment with NH<sub>4</sub>HB<sub>4</sub>O<sub>7</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O at a weight rate of 1:3. The cathode was composed of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C-BN, carbon black and PVDF at the ratio of 7:2:1 in mass. Both anode and cathode were precycled in a half-cell model before proceeding full cell design and the weight ratio of anode and cathode were controlled to be around 6.8:1 with anode mass loading was around 0.8 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The charging/discharging cycling were conducted within 0.5–3.5 V. The specific capacities of electrodes were all calculated based on the total mass of composites. For full cell testing, the specific capacities were calculated based on the total mass of anode.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Detailed synthetic processes of N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs are given in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec2" aria-label="Experimental Section">Experimental Section</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the schematic of the synthetic processes. In brief, the CoS<sub>2</sub>/MCNFs was prepared by the facile solvothermal method employing Co(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O, thiourea, ethylene glycol solution, and MCNFs as carbon substrate. After the solvothermal process, the unique N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs were obtained with ultrathin CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets wrapping the outer surface of the MCNFs and CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles anchoring in the multichannel. The novel structure is expected to deliver a stable hosting and fast transportation passageway for the sodium-ion transfer and repeated intercalation/deintercalation, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, which could perform with good capacity as anode materials for NIBs, just like the artful structure of okra growing in nature achieves efficient absorbtion of nutrients and emission of scrap.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11984p_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Illustration of (a) preparation process for CoS<sub>2</sub>/MCNFs composites; (b) Na<sup><b>+</b></sup> ions insertion and extraction processes. The MCNFs as a robust host is able to efficiently resist mechanical forces and release stresses during the intercalation/deintercalation of sodium ions. Furthermore, double morphologies of CoS<sub>2</sub> (CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets) extremely shorten the electronic transport distances and enhance the sodium ion storage capacity.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11984&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a presents the XRD patterns, in which all peaks are well matched with the CoS<sub>2</sub> phase (JCPDS card No. 41-1471). No other impurities are observed in the XRD spectra for both CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs materials and bare CoS<sub>2</sub> samples (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> of the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes-1" aria-label="Supporting Information">Supporting Information</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a>), therefore confirming the high purity of the synthesized samples. Note that no (002) peak of MCNFs is detected, suggesting that the MCNFs is wrapped around CoS<sub>2</sub> and homogeneously dispersed with a low amount. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Raman spectroscopy is employed to further examine the obtained materials. The two samples, N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs and bare CoS<sub>2</sub> show similar Raman bands at around 286, 314, 388, and 414 cm<sup>–1</sup> as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b, corresponding to the <i>E</i><sub><i>g</i></sub>, <i>T</i><sub>g</sub>(1), <i>A</i><sub><i>g</i></sub>, and <i>T</i><sub>g</sub>(2) modes of the CoS<sub>2</sub> crystal, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a><i>T</i><sub>g</sub>(1) modes are the various combinations of librational and stretching vibration and as for <i>E<sub>g</sub></i>, which is a single librational mode of the dumbbells. The <i>A</i><sub><i>g</i></sub> and <i>T</i><sub>g</sub>(2) modes are assigned to the in-phase and out-of-phase stretching vibrations of the sulfur atoms. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The Raman spectra in the higher wavenumber region (1200–1800 cm<sup>–1</sup>, the inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b) shows the D and G bands, at approximately 1335 cm<sup>–1</sup> and 1599 cm<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. In detail, the D band can be attributed to the structural defects within the carbon layers, and the G band is due to the <i>E</i><sub>2<i>g</i></sub> vibrational mode present in the sp<sup>2</sup>-bonded graphitic carbon atoms, which commonly exist in graphitic structures. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11984p_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD patterns of N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs; (b) Raman results of N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs, pure multichannel carbon nanofibers and bare CoS<sub>2</sub>s; XPS of N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs; (c) survey spectra; (d) Co 2p spectra; (e) S 2p spectra; and (f) N 1s spectra.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11984&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Notably, the intensity ratio (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) in CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs is calculated to be around 1.012, slightly larger than that of pure MCNFs (0.977). The higher <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs indicates that extra structural defects were created by nitrogen atoms incorporating into the MCNFs. The proportion of CoS<sub>2</sub> within the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode is confirmed by TG tests in the air atmosphere (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>) and is calculated to be ∼81.4 wt % based on the reaction: 3CoS<sub>2(s)</sub> + 2O<sub>2(g)</sub> = Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4(s)</sub> + 6S<sub>(g)</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The chemical compositions of the N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs hybrid were identified by XPS testing. The survey spectrum of N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c displays five elements, C, Co, S, N, and O, existing in the sample proving the high purity of as-prepared product. Note that there is a weak peak of O 1s, due to the oxidation tendencies of CoS<sub>2</sub> in the air, similar to that in the literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> The high-resolution spectra of Co 2p is displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d, exhibiting two peaks locating at 794.2 and 779.1 eV and two satellite peaks (denoted as “Sat.”) at around 804.2 and 782.6 eV by fitting of the Co 2p spectra of N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs, derived from spin–orbit of Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> for CoS<sub>2</sub>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e, two different spin–orbit signals and one shakeup satellite peak in the S 2p high resolution spectrum are distinguished locating at 162.6, 163.8, and 168.4 eV, rooted from S 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, S 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, and oxidized S, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The doped S in the composites contributes to enlarge the interlayer distance due to the larger covalent radius of S atoms and improves the electrochemical activity of the conductive matrix, which is thus beneficial for enhancing the Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> Furthermore, the XPS characterization confirms the presence of around 5.11% nitrogen in the composites, consistent with N with two fitted peaks, the pyridinic-N at about 400.7 eV and pyrrolic-N at 398.6 eV, as can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f. These doped-N carbon layers possess high chemical activities, beneficial for sodium kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The morphology and microstructures of pure MCNFs and the as-prepared CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs were tested by FESEM and TEM. An FESEM image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a demonstrates that the pure MCNFs are fibrous with diameters ranging from 0.5 to 0.7 μm, and the TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b) of the MCNFs presents uniform parallel channels in the MCNFs with a narrow size distribution. After a facile solvothermal treatment, the MCNFs were successfully coated with an ultrathin CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet layer as can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c with low magnification. From the TEM image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d, we can vaguely see the parallel channels inside the MCNFs and the CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets layer covering the MCNFs. It should be noticed that there is another morphology, CoS<sub>2</sub> particles, generated in the parallel channels. To clearly show CoS<sub>2</sub> particles and the layout of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs, the CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets layer was peeled off by washing it with a 5 M hydrochloric acid solution along with the ultrasonic treatment. The TEM image of samples after treatment is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>, and the CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles can be seen stably anchored in the parallel channels of MCNFs with a uniform distribution, confirming the double-morphology CoS<sub>2</sub> are formed and anchored on MCNFs. High-magnification of SEM images clearly reveal the cross-sectional shape characteristics (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e) and CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f). From <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e, it is indicated that those parallel channels are retained quite completely in CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs, and the wrinkled CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets layer with an average thickness of 180 nm grew stably at the outer surface. Impressively, the CoS<sub>2</sub> layer consists of an extremely paper-thin CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet at 1–5 nm thick (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f) stacked desultorily, and it can be expected that CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites with unique double-morphology CoS<sub>2</sub> structure can enhance the sodium ion storage performance.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11984p_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) SEM images and (b) TEM images of pure multichannel carbon nanofibers (MCNFs); (c) SEM images and (d) TEM images of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites; and (e and f) high magnification SEM images of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11984&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">A representative high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image clearly reveals an interplanar distance of 0.248 nm in the lattice fringes, matching well with the (210) planes of cubic CoS<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). The similar synthesis process without added MCNFs produces bare CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles with a rough texture, which is constructed by interconnected nanoparticles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a>). The distinct micro-morphologies highlight the role that MCNFs serving as solid phase substrates, which provide the active sites for CoS<sub>2</sub> suppressing the structural agglomeration of the effective substances during the growth of the CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet structure. The energy dispersive spectrometer mapping of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b confirms that Co, S, and C elements were distributed uniformly among the composites. The results discussed above indicated that the double-morphology CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs, with multichannel carbon nanofibers as a central axis, CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets as coats surrounding the axis, and CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles as the filler in the channels, are successfully designed.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11984p_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) HRTEM image and (b) energy dispersive spectrometer mapping of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11984&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To observe the electrochemical behavior of both CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode and bare CoS<sub>2</sub> electrode, 2032-type coin cells were assembled. Effect of various electrolytes were first investigated. The charge/discharge behavior of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrodes using 1.0 M NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> in EC/DEC (ethylene carbonate and diethyl carbonate), DEGDME (diethyleneglcol dimethyl ether), and PC (propylene carbonate) at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> between 0.4 to 2.9 V are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. The result clearly indicates that CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode with DEGDME exhibits a larger capacity retention and smaller voltage polarization than that with EC/DEC and PC electrolytes, which can be ascribed to three critical factor: (1) a more stable SEI (solid-electrolyte interface) forming on CoS<sub>2</sub>, (2) much faster charge-transfer kinetics DEGDME, and (3) better wettability among electrode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref20 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref20 ref46" aria-label="reference 7, 20 and 46">(7,20,46)</a> The representative discharging/charging voltage profiles using both electrodes with 1.0 M NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> in DEGDME between 0.4 and 2.9 V are depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, b. The discharge–charge curves in both cases show a similar trend, in which discharge plateaus near 1.6–1.3 V and 1.1–0.8 V and charge plateaus near 1.8–1.5 Vand 2.1–1.8 V are clearly observed, demonstrating that the introduction of MCNFs cannot change the electrochemical mechanism of CoS<sub>2</sub>. When cycled at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs provide an initial discharge/charge capacity of 849.59/681.37 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a high Coulombic efficiency 80.2%. Along charge/discharge cycling processes, the charge capacity of the 10<sup>th</sup> cycle grew slightly to 712.26 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and the phenomenon of capacity increase of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs for the first 10 cycles was observed, which can be ascribed to an activation and stabilization stage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref47" aria-label="reference 34 and 47">(34,47)</a> As for the bare CoS<sub>2</sub> electrode, the initial discharge/charge capacity was 717.61/547.37 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, resulting in a comparatively low Coulombic efficiency of 76.3%. Furthermore there was an obvious rapid capacity fading trend in the voltage profiles for the CoS<sub>2</sub> electrode which led to an as low as 419.02 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> charge capacity at the 10<sup>th</sup> cycle. And conversely, that of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode was overlapping, indicating good cycle stability. To further investigate the discharge/charge behaviors of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites, CV curves were obtained at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, two reduction peaks were located at 0.7 and 1.5 V in the initial cathodic sweep. The peak at 1.5 V was attributed to the initial insertion of sodium, and the broad peak at 0.7 V was due to the displacement reactions to the formations of Co, Na<sub>2</sub>S, and SEI. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> Accordingly, oxidation peaks at 1.8 and 2.1 V were detected, which were related to the formation of CoS<sub>2</sub>. From the second cycle onward, the main reduction peaks stabilized at 0.8 and 1.6 V, due to the improved kinetics of the electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The CV curve of the bare CoS<sub>2</sub> at the second cycle is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. With a low scan rate, one strong and sharp reduction peak at around 1.56 V can be detected, and two weak and broad reduction peaks (Peak 2 and Peak 3 in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a>) at 0.7 and 1.2 V also can be observed. However, no obvious discharging plateaus at 0.7 and 1.2 V are observed in the discharging curves of bare CoS<sub>2</sub> in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a>. In fact, a very weak and short discharging plateau also can be found near 0.7 and 1.2 V in the first cycles, however, the signals disappear when cycled after the second lap, which is due to the serious dissolution and diffusion of sulfides into the electrolyte and formation of insulated sodium sulfide covering on the anode. These all increase the cell’s internal resistance and polarization, which could render the voltage plateau near 0.7 and 1.2 V gradually vanished, eventually being covered up by robust signal. A similar phenomenon can be observed in the conventional Li/S cell. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, b, the discharging voltage (in the initial three discharging-charging cycles) of bare CoS<sub>2</sub> is higher than that of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrodes, which may be due to the better surface wettability of bare CoS<sub>2</sub> with electrolyte than that of the multichannel carbon matrix. However, with the cycling proceeding, a portion of sulfide is transformed into sodium sulfide and deposited on the anode, thus resulting in an increase of the internal cell resistance due to the insulating properties of sodium sulfide. Therefore, the discharging voltage of bare CoS<sub>2</sub> decreases and increases the charging voltage. This becomes obvious when it is discharged at 10<sup>th</sup> cycle in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. The discharging voltage of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs is higher than that of bare CoS<sub>2</sub>, which confirms that the multichannel carbon matrix can do a great favor in restraining the sulfide dissolution and ensuring long cycling stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11984p_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Discharge/charge behaviors of (a) CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode and (b) bare CoS<sub>2</sub>. (c) Cyclic voltammograms curves of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs at a low sweep rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (d) EIS (electrochemical impedance spectra) and (e) cycling life testing of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11984&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In addition, to analyze electrochemical behaviors of both electrodes, electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) were employed and are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d. The plot was fitted and presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d. In the equivalent circuit, <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> is related to Warburg impedance, reflecting the Na-ions diffusion process in the bulk electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref50" aria-label="reference 39 and 50">(39,50)</a> Apparently, the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode shows a lower postcycling charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> = 31.45 Ω) compared with that of the bare CoS<sub>2</sub> electrode (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> = 75.92 Ω), which can be attributed to the improved electrochemical reaction kinetics owing to the conducting effect of MCNFs. Consequently, all of these account for a better sodium storage property and a higher reversible capacity than that of the CoS<sub>2</sub> electrode. To better illustrate the structural superiority of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs serving as sodium storage anode materials, the cycling life of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode along with bare CoS<sub>2</sub> were investigated at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs provides a discharging capacity of 539.28 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at first cycle, which slightly rises in the following cycles, reaching up to 620 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 60 cycles and surprisingly still stabilized at around 620 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 900 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e). This is mainly due to the fact that extra vacancies and structural defects on MCNFs are generated as the cycle number increases, thus enhancing the insertion of far more Na<sup>+</sup> ions. On the contrary, the capacity of the bare CoS<sub>2</sub> decreases rapidly to 337 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> only after 100 cycles, much less than the remnant capacity of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs (620 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), further proving the good long-term electrochemical stability of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode. In addition, the contribution of pure MCNFs is also investigated by galvanostatic charge/discharge testing, and the representative discharge/charge voltage profiles are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. The reversible specific capacity of pure MCNFs is only around 37.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which is negligible. Thus, the rate of reversible capacity (based on the mass of CoS<sub>2</sub> in the composites) to the CoS<sub>2</sub> theoretical capacity is calculated to be about 86.4%, which demonstrates that CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs has superior electrochemical reaction kinetics. The loss of irreversibility observed could be due to the slight dissolution of the reaction intermediate sulfide from the surface of the anode into the electrolyte.</div><div class="NLM_p">Rate performances were studied under adjoining cycles of various current densities for the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a,b. It is observed from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a that the discharging/charging profiles show limited changes from a low current density 0.1 to a high current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating a low polarization of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs and fast electrochemical reaction kinetics. Furthermore, the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode delivers reversible capacities of 711.52, 681.91, 641.94, 610.20, and 569.43 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the corresponding current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b). Even under a current density with the 50-fold increase, a charge capacity of 508.68 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> can still be kept at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. When the current density back to the initial value of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the electrode provides a reversible capacity of 690.71 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and remains stable very well. To visually view the superiority of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs’ unique nanostructure for the sodium storage, a comparison between the Na-storage performance with previously reported CoS<sub>2</sub> electrode materials is collected in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. The high specific capacity (712.26 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>) as well as an outstanding cycling stability (620 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 900 cycles) that the hybrid CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode delivered from the experimental tests are the highest compared with those reported materials in the literature. To further demonstrate the advantage of as-prepared double-morphology CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs, its rate performance and long-cycling stability are compared with single-morphology CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs, which has been reported in our previous work. As can be seen in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a, b</a>, single-morphology CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites are composed with CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles anchoring on MCNFs. The discharge capacity of single-morphology CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs is around 529, 485, 404, 312, 288, and 249 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the corresponding current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5A g<sup>–1</sup> (in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8c</a>), which is lower than that of the double-morphology CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs, respectively. Although the single-morphology CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode has a higher cycling life than that of the double-morphology one, the double-morphology CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode has an advantage in the higher reversible capacity as presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8d</a>. The excellent electrochemical performance that the double-morphology CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs demonstrate can be ascribed to the synergy between CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets on the surface and CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles inside the channels. On the one hand, the nanosheets on the surface of MCNFs are in favor of lowering the barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> inserting/extracting, therefore reducing the charge transfer resistance. More importantly, the unique wrinkled nanosheets are in favor of providing a better wettability between electrodes and electrolytes than other structures, such as nanoparticles and polyhedron, especially in the initial stage for electrolyte infiltration. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 51 and 52">(51,52)</a> On the other hand, regarding the nanoparticles in the multi-channels of carbon nanofibers, CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles have more robust construction than CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. Furthermore, CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles possess a higher volumetric energy density than nanosheets, which can enormously help in improving the energy density of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites. In general, the as-prepared double-morphology CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs have the unique structural advantages and improved surface effects, resulting in enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11984p_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a, b) Rate performance of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode at a series of current densities of 0.1 to 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11984&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To comprehensively clarify the reversible Na-ions stoargae mechanism of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs, HRTEM and ex situ XRD were investigated within the two potential ranges of 1.0–2.9 V and 0.4–2.9 V. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a exhibits the XRD patterns of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode after 10 cycles at two different charge/discharge states at 1.0 and 2.9 V, among which signals at around 21.5° and 23.9° derive from parafilm, which is used to protect the materials from the air. Except for the peaks of the parafilm and MCNFs (at 26.3°), the characteristic peaks at 32.4° and 36.3° of the CoS<sub>2</sub> were detected in both the discharge state at 1.0 V and charge state at 2.9 V, while no diffraction peak of the Na<sub>2</sub>S and Co were detected. This indicates that no Na<sub>2</sub>S and Co crystalline phase was formed within this potential state of 1.0 V. In the meantime, this also confirms the insertion of the Na<sup>+</sup> ions process and the formation of intermediate Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoS<sub>2</sub> phase in the discharging process. The CoS<sub>2</sub> characteristics peaks can be clearly observed in XRD patterns of charged status at 2.9 V in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a and matches well to the standards card for cubic phase CoS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS card No. 41-1471), which indicates that there is limited influence on the original structure of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs when it is in the charging process with repeating Na-ions insertion/extraction. For further investigation, the potential window was adjusted to 0.4–2.9 V, and the ex situ XRD testing results of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrodes after 10 cycles are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b. After having been fully discharged to 0.4 V, the XRD signals of Co and Na<sub>2</sub>S were finally found with the peaks locating at 44.8° (Co, JCPDS No. 88-2325) and 39.2°(Na<sub>2</sub>S, JCPDS No. 47-1698), respectively. This indicated that the conversion reaction from Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoS<sub>2</sub> phase to Na<sub>2</sub>S and Co occurred below 1.0 V with the consecutive Na-ions insertion process. When it was charged back to 2.9 V, one weak but sharp characteristic peak of CoS<sub>2</sub> can be detected at 32.8°, confirming a superior reversibility of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs’ Na-ions inserting/extracting reactions. The HRTEM images were also employed to clarify the electrochemical reaction mechanism of CoS<sub>2</sub> as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b, c. When discharged to 0.4 V, the lattice with an interplanar spacing of 0.20 and 0.23 nm are well consistent with the (111) crystal plane of Co and (220) crystal plane of Na<sub>2</sub>S, respectively. As fully charged to 2.9 V, the (101) plane of CoS<sub>2</sub> appears, further demonstrating the process of initial insertion of Na-ions and typical conversion reaction at low potential window. This coincides well with the analysis gained from the CV curves of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, as well as XRD patterns in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b, c. The reaction of Na-ions storage mechanism for CoS<sub>2</sub> can be deduced as follows:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11984p_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span></div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11984p_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Ex situ XRD patterns (a) between 1.0 and 2.9 V, and ex situ XRD patterns (b) and HRTEM images (c, d) between 0.4 and 2.9 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11984&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To realize the industry applications of using the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs as an electrode and to demonstrate its excellent electrochemical performance, the full batteries were assembled using CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C-BN (XRD and SEM patterns showed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>) as electrodes. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a shows the construction of the full batteries, including the anode, cathode, a separator, and the NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>-based electrolyte. The Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C-BN cathode materials were fabricated by a facile self-assembly process, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> showing a discharging capacity of 103.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (when cycled at 1 C) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S10 and S11</a>). The active materials of the cathode and anode were equilibrated with a mass ratio of 6.8:1. The cycling performance at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> between 0.4 and 3.5 V is depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b. It shows that the discharge capacity at the 10<sup>th</sup> cycle is 356 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of the anode. A capacity of 348 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is obtained after 100 cycles, demonstrating its excellent cyclability. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a> shows the charge/discharge curves of the full cell at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> at the 1<sup>st</sup>, 5<sup>th</sup>, 50<sup>th</sup>, and 100<sup>th</sup> cycles between 0.5 and 3.5 V. The initial discharging and charging capacities are 418 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>and 521 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> calculated based on the total mass of the anode. The irreversible capacity loss at the initial cycle could be caused by the electrolyte decomposition and SEI formation. With on-going cycling, the full cell stabilized, and a reversible capacity of around 348 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was obtained even after 100 cycles, presenting excellent cycling stability. In addition, the full batteries were further tested to run a light emitting diode (LED) (in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b). Remarkable performance and cyclability for sodium storage was observed using the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrode, mainly because of the electrodes unique nanostructure with multichannel carbon nanofibers as a central axis and CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets as a coating surrounding it. This performance and cyclability is achieved for several reasons. First, the multichannel carbon nanofibers serve not merely as an outstanding conductive framework in favor of fast charge transmission but also as a robust substrate for well dispersing and immobilizing CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, thus strengthening the structural stability of the hybrid materials. Second, CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets with an ultrathin feature at the MCNFs surface and CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles in the different channels increase the contact areas with the electrolyte as well as the electrochemical effective sites, which are beneficial for rapid diffusion and storage of Na-ions. Finally, the unique structure offers sufficient free spaces, which could efficiently buffer the stress during repeated cycling, resulting in an improvement of both capacity retention and rate performance.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11984p_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Electrochemical testing of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C-BN sodium-ion full cell: (a) Illustration of full cell structure; and (b) Long cycling performance of full cell at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The photo in (b) shows the full cell which powers a white LED.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/asset/images/large/am-2018-11984p_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11984&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">An innovative CoS<sub>2</sub>/multichannel carbon nanofiber composite (CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs) hybrid material with elaborately controlled novel configurations was successfully developed through electrospinning followed by a facile solvothermal method. The well-defined ultrathin CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles are uniformly grown on MCNF networks with powerful bonding force, which offers an optimized host for Na-ion inserting/extracting reactions. It was surprising that when using it as an anode for NIBs, the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs exhibited a high specific capacity, outstanding rate capacity, and excellent cycle life. The three-dimension conductive framework of MCNFs, double-morphology structure of ultrathin CoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, and nanosized CoS<sub>2</sub> particles, as well as the unique architectural configuration and heteroatom doping, account for the extraordinary electrochemical performance of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs. This novel nanostructure delivers good insights for Na-ion storage electrodes and even sheds some light on other relevant functional nanophase materials for Li-ion batteries and ultracapacitors.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.8b11984" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.8b11984</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD pattern of the bare CoS<sub>2</sub>; TGA curves of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites and bare MCNFs; TEM image of treated CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites with HCl solution; SEM images of bare CoS<sub>2</sub>; charge–discharge curves of CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs electrodes using 1 M NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> in EC/DEC, PC, DEGDME at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and cycling performance in the three different solvents; CV curves at 2<sup>nd</sup> cycle with three reduction peaks marked as Peak 1, Peak 2, and Peak 3 (in a scan rate of 0.07 mV/s) and charge–discharge curves (at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>) of bare CoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes; SEM and TEM of single-morphology N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites, reported in the literature; rate performance and long-term cycling performance (at a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>) of single-morphology N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites and double-morphology N-doped CoS<sub>2</sub>@MCNFs composites in this work; and comparison of the Na-storage performance with reported CoS<sub>2</sub> electrode materials (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11984/suppl_file/am8b11984_si_001.pdf">am8b11984_si_001.pdf (2.43 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b11984" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Target construction of ultrathin graphitic carbon encapsulated FeS hierarchical microspheres featuring superior low-temperature lithium/sodium storage properties</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fan, Hong-Hong; Li, Huan-Huan; Guo, Jin-Zhi; Zheng, Yan-Ping; Huang, Ke-Cheng; Fan, Chao-Ying; Sun, Hai-Zhu; Li, Xi-Fei; Wu, Xing-Long; Zhang, Jing-Ping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7997-8005</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although numerous electrode materials based on conversion-reactions have been investigated for lithium/sodium ion batteries (L/SIBs), the low-temp. performance is still a big challenge for their practical application in cold climates.  Herein, every individual small FeS sphere wrapped by several graphitic carbon layers in a micro-nano system (FeS@g-C) is designed and explored.  The in situ generated FeS@g-C delivers excellent electrochem. performance over a wide temp. range.  For instance, the reversible capacity of FeS@g-C can reach 562 mA h/g at 0.2 A/g for LIBs at -20 °C.  And for SIBs, it exhibits a capacity of 311 mA h/g at 0.05 A/g even when operated at a cryogenic temp. of -25 °C.  In addn., when paired with a Na3V2(PO4)2O2F cathode as a full cell for SIBs, it also works well, revealing great application prospects.  This good electrochem. performance of the FeS@g-C can be attributed to the short ion-diffusion paths provided by the ultrasmall FeS nanospheres along with the more important g-C coating, which guarantees the high cond. of FeS@g-C.  In addn., the perfectly shaped and porous hierarchical FeS spheres can effectively accommodate the vol. variation during electrochem. processes, thus endowing the FeS@g-C with a robust structural stability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoxvCqdhbf9RrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liCxOaIERCwuQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXmsFyqsbw%253D&amp;md5=b76305f77cbce481f0e053cc45d65c08</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b04866"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Dodecahedron-Shaped Porous Vanadium Oxide and Carbon Composite for High-Rate Lithium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Dodecahedron-Shaped Porous Vanadium Oxide and Carbon Composite for High-Rate Lithium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Yifang; Pan, Anqiang; Wang, Yaping; Wei, Weifeng; Su, Yanhui; Hu, Jimei; Cao, Guozhong; Liang, Shuquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
